<p><b>Djibouti</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/dʒ/ 'j' in 'jam'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">dʒ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ɪ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ˈ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'b' in 'buy'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">b</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/uː/ long 'oo' in 'food'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">uː</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'t' in 'tie'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">t</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/i/ 'y' in 'happy'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">i</span></a></span><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span></span> <span title="English pronunciation respelling" class="Unicode"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Wikipedia:Pronunciation respelling key"><i>ji-<b><span class="smallcaps"><span class="SMALLCAPS" style="FONT-VARIANT:SMALL-CAPS;"><span class="NOCAPS" style="TEXT-TRANSFORM:LOWERCASE;">BOO</span></span></span></b>-tee</i></a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar">جيبوتي</span>‎ <i><span title="ar ALA transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">Jībūtī</span></i>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <span lang="fr"><i>Djibouti</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Somali_language" title="Somali language">Somali</a>: <span lang="so"><i>Jabuuti</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Afar_language" title="Afar language">Afar</a>: <span lang="aa"><i>Gabuuti</i></span>), officially the <b>Republic of Djibouti</b>, is a country located in the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>. It is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a> in the north, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> in the west and south, and <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a> in the southeast. The remainder of the border is formed by the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Aden" title="Gulf of Aden">Gulf of Aden</a> at the east. Djibouti occupies a total area of just 23,200&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (8,958&#160;sq&#160;mi).</p>
<p>In antiquity, the territory was part of the <a href="/wiki/Land_of_Punt" title="Land of Punt">Land of Punt</a>. Nearby <a href="/wiki/Zeila" title="Zeila">Zeila</a> (now in <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>) was the seat of the medieval <a href="/wiki/Adal_Sultanate" title="Adal Sultanate">Adal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ifat_Sultanate" title="Ifat Sultanate" class="mw-redirect">Ifat</a> Sultanates. In the late 19th century, the colony of <a href="/wiki/French_Somaliland" title="French Somaliland">French Somaliland</a> was established following treaties signed by the ruling <a href="/wiki/Somali_people" title="Somali people" class="mw-redirect">Somali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afar_people" title="Afar people">Afar</a> sultans with the French<sup id="cite_ref-Uwechue_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uwechue-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Apcoatf_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apcoatf-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> and its <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Ethiopian_Railway" title="Imperial Ethiopian Railway" class="mw-redirect">railroad</a> to <a href="/wiki/Dire_Dawa" title="Dire Dawa">Dire Dawa</a> (and later <a href="/wiki/Addis_Ababa" title="Addis Ababa">Addis Ababa</a>) allowed it to quickly supersede <a href="/wiki/Zeila" title="Zeila">Zeila</a> as the port for southern <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ogaden" title="Ogaden">Ogaden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EB_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> It was subsequently renamed to the <a href="/wiki/French_Territory_of_the_Afars_and_the_Issas" title="French Territory of the Afars and the Issas">French Territory of the Afars and the Issas</a> in 1967. A decade later, the Djiboutian people voted for independence. This officially marked the establishment of the <i>Republic of Djibouti</i>, named after its <a href="/wiki/Djibouti_(city)" title="Djibouti (city)">capital city</a>. Djibouti joined the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> the same year, on September 20, 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> In the early 1990s, tensions over government representation led to armed conflict, which ended in a power sharing agreement in 2000 between the ruling party and the opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Djibouti is a multi-ethnic nation with a population of over 810,000 inhabitants. The Somali and Afar make up the two largest ethnic groups. Both speak <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic languages</a>, which serve as recognized national languages. <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> constitute the country's two official languages. About 94% of residents adhere to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, a religion that has been predominant in the region for more than 1,000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Djibouti is strategically located near the world's busiest shipping lanes, controlling access to the Red Sea and <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>. It serves as a key refueling and transshipment center, and is the principal maritime port for imports to and exports from neighboring Ethiopia. A burgeoning commercial hub, the nation is the site of various foreign military bases, including <a href="/wiki/Camp_Lemonnier" title="Camp Lemonnier">Camp Lemonnier</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Authority_on_Development" title="Intergovernmental Authority on Development">Intergovernmental Authority on Development</a> regional body also has its headquarters in Djibouti City.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Djibouti" title="History of Djibouti">History of Djibouti</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pottery_of_Asa_Koma.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Pottery_of_Asa_Koma.JPG/170px-Pottery_of_Asa_Koma.JPG" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Pottery_of_Asa_Koma.JPG/255px-Pottery_of_Asa_Koma.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Pottery_of_Asa_Koma.JPG/340px-Pottery_of_Asa_Koma.JPG 2x" data-file-width="612" data-file-height="766" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pottery_of_Asa_Koma.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Geometric design pottery found in <a href="/wiki/Asa_Koma" title="Asa Koma">Asa Koma</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Djibouti area has been inhabited since at least the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a>. According to linguists, the first <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afro-Asiatic</a>-speaking populations arrived in the region during this period from the family's proposed <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_Urheimat" title="Afroasiatic Urheimat">urheimat</a> ("original homeland") in the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Valley" title="Nile Valley" class="mw-redirect">Nile Valley</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> or the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Other scholars propose that the Afro-Asiatic family developed in situ in the Horn, with its speakers subsequently dispersing from there.<sup id="cite_ref-Blench143144_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blench143144-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Pottery predating the mid-2nd millennium has been found at <a href="/wiki/Asa_Koma" title="Asa Koma">Asa Koma</a>, an inland lake area on the Gobaad Plain. The site's ware is characterized by punctate and incision geometric designs, which bear a similarity to the Sabir culture phase 1 ceramics from Ma'layba in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Arabia" title="Southern Arabia" class="mw-redirect">Southern Arabia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ohvah_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ohvah-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> Long-horned humpless cattle bones have likewise been discovered at Asa Koma, suggesting that domesticated cattle was present by around 3,500 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-Connah_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Connah-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> Rock art of what appear to be antelopes and a giraffe are also found at <a href="/wiki/Dorra" title="Dorra">Dorra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Balho" title="Balho">Balho</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Amvjaa_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amvjaa-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Additionally, between Djibouti City and Loyada are a number of <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphism" title="Anthropomorphism">anthropomorphic</a> and phallic <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stelae</a>. The structures are associated with graves of rectangular shape flanked by vertical slabs, as also found in central <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>. The Djibouti-Loyada stelae are of uncertain age, and some of them are adorned with a T-shaped symbol.<sup id="cite_ref-Fattovich_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fattovich-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Together with northern <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> coast of <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>, Djibouti is considered the most likely location of the territory known to the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian" title="Ancient Egyptian" class="mw-redirect">Ancient Egyptians</a> as <i><a href="/wiki/Land_of_Punt" title="Land of Punt">Punt</a></i> (or <i>Ta Netjeru,</i> meaning "God's Land"). The first mention of the Land of Punt dates to the 25th century BC.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> The Puntites were a nation of people who had close relations with <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> during the times of <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">Pharaoh</a> <a href="/wiki/Sahure" title="Sahure">Sahure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Queen_regnant" title="Queen regnant">Queen</a> <a href="/wiki/Hatshepsut" title="Hatshepsut">Hatshepsut</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> According to the temple reliefs at <a href="/wiki/Deir_el-Bahari" title="Deir el-Bahari">Deir el-Bahari</a>, the Land of Punt was ruled at that time by King Parahu and Queen Ati.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ifat.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Ifat.png/220px-Ifat.png" width="220" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Ifat.png/330px-Ifat.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Ifat.png/440px-Ifat.png 2x" data-file-width="485" data-file-height="500" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ifat.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Ifat_Sultanate" title="Ifat Sultanate" class="mw-redirect">Ifat Sultanate</a>'s realm in the 14th century.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Through close contacts with the adjacent <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> for more than 1,000 years, the <a href="/wiki/Somali_people" title="Somali people" class="mw-redirect">Somali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afar_people" title="Afar people">Afar</a> ethnic groups in the region became among the first populations on the continent to embrace Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ifat_Sultanate" title="Ifat Sultanate" class="mw-redirect">Ifat Sultanate</a> was a medieval kingdom in the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>. Founded in 1285 by the <a href="/wiki/Walashma_dynasty" title="Walashma dynasty">Walashma dynasty</a>, it was centered in <a href="/wiki/Zeila" title="Zeila">Zeila</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> Ifat established bases in Djibouti and northern Somalia, and from there expanded southward to the <a href="/wiki/Ahmar_Mountains" title="Ahmar Mountains">Ahmar Mountains</a>. Its <a href="/wiki/Sultan" title="Sultan">Sultan</a> Umar Walashma (or his son Ali, according to another source) is recorded as having conquered the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Showa" title="Sultanate of Showa">Sultanate of Shewa</a> in 1285. Taddesse Tamrat explains Sultan Umar's military expedition as an effort to consolidate the Muslim territories in the Horn, in much the same way as Emperor <a href="/wiki/Yekuno_Amlak" title="Yekuno Amlak">Yekuno Amlak</a> was attempting to unite the Christian territories in the highlands during the same period. These two states inevitably came into conflict over Shewa and territories further south. A lengthy war ensued, but the Muslim sultanates of the time were not strongly unified. Ifat was finally defeated by Emperor <a href="/wiki/Amda_Seyon_I" title="Amda Seyon I">Amda Seyon I</a> of Ethiopia in 1332, and withdrew from Shewa.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Obock_1890.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Obock_1890.png/220px-Obock_1890.png" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Obock_1890.png/330px-Obock_1890.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Obock_1890.png/440px-Obock_1890.png 2x" data-file-width="1419" data-file-height="1035" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Obock_1890.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Obock" title="Obock">Obock</a> in <a href="/wiki/French_Somaliland" title="French Somaliland">French Somaliland</a>, circa 1890.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From 1862 until 1894, the land to the north of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Tadjoura" title="Gulf of Tadjoura">Gulf of Tadjoura</a> was called <i>Obock</i> and was ruled by Somali and Afar Sultans, local authorities with whom France signed various treaties between 1883 and 1887 to first gain a foothold in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-Uwechue_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uwechue-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Apcoatf_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apcoatf-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chisholm_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chisholm-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1894, <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9once_Lagarde" title="Léonce Lagarde">Léonce Lagarde</a> established a permanent French administration in the <a href="/wiki/Djibouti_(city)" title="Djibouti (city)">city of Djibouti</a> and named the region <a href="/wiki/French_Somaliland" title="French Somaliland">French Somaliland</a>. It lasted from 1896 until 1967, when it was renamed the <i>Territoire Français des Afars et des Issas</i> (TFAI) ("<a href="/wiki/French_Territory_of_the_Afars_and_the_Issas" title="French Territory of the Afars and the Issas">French Territory of the Afars and the Issas</a>").<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1958, on the eve of neighboring Somalia's independence in 1960, a <a href="/wiki/French_Somaliland_overseas_territory_referendum,_1958" title="French Somaliland overseas territory referendum, 1958" class="mw-redirect">referendum</a> was held in Djibouti to decide whether to join the Somali Republic or to remain with France. The referendum turned out in favour of a continued association with France, partly due to a combined yes vote by the sizable Afar ethnic group and resident Europeans.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrington2006_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrington2006-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> There were also allegations of widespread <a href="/wiki/Vote_rigging" title="Vote rigging" class="mw-redirect">vote rigging</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> The majority of those who had voted no were Somalis who were strongly in favour of joining a united Somalia as had been proposed by <a href="/wiki/Mahmoud_Harbi" title="Mahmoud Harbi">Mahmoud Harbi</a>, Vice President of the Government Council. Harbi was killed in a plane crash two years later.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrington2006_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrington2006-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Djibouti_Ville.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Djibouti_Ville.jpg/220px-Djibouti_Ville.jpg" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Djibouti_Ville.jpg/330px-Djibouti_Ville.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Djibouti_Ville.jpg/440px-Djibouti_Ville.jpg 2x" data-file-width="520" data-file-height="378" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Djibouti_Ville.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Aerial view of <a href="/wiki/Djibouti_(city)" title="Djibouti (city)">Djibouti City</a>, the capital of Djibouti.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1967, a <a href="/wiki/French_Somaliland_independence_referendum,_1967" title="French Somaliland independence referendum, 1967">second plebiscite</a> was held to determine the fate of the territory. Initial results supported a continued but looser relationship with France. Voting was also divided along ethnic lines, with the resident Somalis generally voting for independence, with the goal of eventual union with Somalia, and the Afars largely opting to remain associated with France.<sup id="cite_ref-Apcoatf_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apcoatf-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> However, the referendum was again marred by reports of vote rigging on the part of the French authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-Aufsnas_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aufsnas-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> Shortly after the referendum was held, the former <i>Côte française des Somalis</i> (French Somaliland) was renamed to <i>Territoire français des Afars et des Issas</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tioipeami_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tioipeami-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1977, a <a href="/wiki/Afars_and_Issas_independence_referendum,_1977" title="Afars and Issas independence referendum, 1977">third referendum</a> took place. A landslide 98.8% of the electorate supported disengagement from France, officially marking Djibouti's independence.<sup id="cite_ref-Nwvol_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nwvol-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hassan_Gouled_Aptidon" title="Hassan Gouled Aptidon">Hassan Gouled Aptidon</a>, a Somali politician who had campaigned for a yes vote in the referendum of 1958, eventually wound up as the nation's first president (1977–1999).<sup id="cite_ref-Barrington2006_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrington2006-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>During its first year, Djibouti joined the <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_African_Unity" title="Organization of African Unity" class="mw-redirect">Organization of African Unity</a> (now the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>. In 1986, the nascent republic was also among the founding members of the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Authority_on_Development" title="Intergovernmental Authority on Development">Intergovernmental Authority on Development</a> regional development organization.</p>
<p>In the early 1990s, tensions over government representation led to armed conflict between Djibouti's ruling <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Rally_for_Progress" title="People's Rally for Progress">People's Rally for Progress</a> (PRP) party and the <a href="/wiki/Front_for_the_Restoration_of_Unity_and_Democracy" title="Front for the Restoration of Unity and Democracy">Front for the Restoration of Unity and Democracy</a> (FRUD) opposition group. The impasse ended in a power sharing agreement in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Djibouti" title="Politics of Djibouti">Politics of Djibouti</a></div>
<div class="hatnote boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/Elections_in_Djibouti" title="Elections in Djibouti">Elections in Djibouti</a></div>
<p>Djibouti is a <a href="/wiki/Semi-presidential_republic" title="Semi-presidential republic" class="mw-redirect">semi-presidential</a> republic, with <a href="/wiki/Executive_power" title="Executive power" class="mw-redirect">executive power</a> resting in the central government, and <a href="/wiki/Legislative_power" title="Legislative power" class="mw-redirect">legislative power</a> in both the government and the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(Djibouti)" title="National Assembly (Djibouti)">Djiboutian National Assembly</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Governance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ismail_Omar_Guelleh_2010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Ismail_Omar_Guelleh_2010.jpg/170px-Ismail_Omar_Guelleh_2010.jpg" width="170" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Ismail_Omar_Guelleh_2010.jpg/255px-Ismail_Omar_Guelleh_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Ismail_Omar_Guelleh_2010.jpg/340px-Ismail_Omar_Guelleh_2010.jpg 2x" data-file-width="948" data-file-height="1332" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ismail_Omar_Guelleh_2010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
President of Djibouti, <a href="/wiki/Isma%C3%AFl_Omar_Guelleh" title="Ismaïl Omar Guelleh">Ismaïl Omar Guelleh</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/President_of_Djibouti" title="President of Djibouti" class="mw-redirect">President</a>, currently <a href="/wiki/Isma%C3%AFl_Omar_Guelleh" title="Ismaïl Omar Guelleh">Ismaïl Omar Guelleh</a>, is the pre-eminent figure in Djiboutian politics; the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commander-in-chief" title="Commander-in-chief">commander-in-chief</a>. The President shares executive power with his or her appointee, the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Djibouti" title="Prime Minister of Djibouti" class="mw-redirect">Prime Minister</a>, currently <a href="/wiki/Abdoulkader_Kamil_Mohamed" title="Abdoulkader Kamil Mohamed">Abdoulkader Kamil Mohamed</a>. The Council of Ministers (<a href="/wiki/Cabinet_(government)" title="Cabinet (government)">cabinet</a>) is responsible to the legislature and presided over by the President.</p>
<p>The judicial system consists of courts of first instance, a High Court of Appeal, and a Supreme Court. The <a href="/wiki/Legal_system" title="Legal system" class="mw-redirect">legal system</a> is a blend of <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">French civil law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a> (Islamic law) and <a href="/wiki/Customary_law" title="Customary law" class="mw-redirect">customary law</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Xeer" title="Xeer">Xeer</a></i>) of the Somali and Afar peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-constitution_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-constitution-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The National Assembly (formerly the <i>Chamber of Deputies</i>) is the country's <a href="/wiki/Legislature" title="Legislature">legislature</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-constitution_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-constitution-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> consisting of 65 members elected every five years.<sup id="cite_ref-freedomhouse_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-freedomhouse-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Unicameral" title="Unicameral" class="mw-redirect">unicameral</a>, the Constitution provides for the creation of a Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-constitution_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-constitution-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dileita_Mohamed_Dileita_detail_090114-F-3682S-269.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Dileita_Mohamed_Dileita_detail_090114-F-3682S-269.jpg/170px-Dileita_Mohamed_Dileita_detail_090114-F-3682S-269.jpg" width="170" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Dileita_Mohamed_Dileita_detail_090114-F-3682S-269.jpg/255px-Dileita_Mohamed_Dileita_detail_090114-F-3682S-269.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Dileita_Mohamed_Dileita_detail_090114-F-3682S-269.jpg/340px-Dileita_Mohamed_Dileita_detail_090114-F-3682S-269.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1046" data-file-height="1212" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dileita_Mohamed_Dileita_detail_090114-F-3682S-269.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Dileita_Mohamed_Dileita" title="Dileita Mohamed Dileita">Dileita Mohamed Dileita</a>, former Prime Minister of Djibouti, Vice-President of the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Rally_for_Progress" title="People's Rally for Progress">People's Rally for Progress</a> (RPP), and President of the Union for a Presidential Majority (UMP).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Djiboutian_parliamentary_election,_2013" title="Djiboutian parliamentary election, 2013">last election</a> was held on 22 February 2013. Djibouti is a <a href="/wiki/Dominant-party_system" title="Dominant-party system">one party dominant state</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Rally_for_Progress" title="People's Rally for Progress">People's Rally for Progress</a> (RPP) controlling the legislature and the executive since its foundation in 1979 (the party currently rules as a part of the <a href="/wiki/Union_for_a_Presidential_Majority" title="Union for a Presidential Majority" class="mw-redirect">Union for a Presidential Majority</a>, which holds all seats). Opposition parties are allowed limited freedom, but the main opposition party, the <a href="/wiki/Union_for_National_Salvation" title="Union for National Salvation">Union for National Salvation</a>, boycotted the 2005 and 2008 elections, citing government control of the media and repression of the opposition candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-freedomhouse_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-freedomhouse-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The government is dominated by the Somali <a href="/wiki/Issa_(clan)" title="Issa (clan)">Issa</a> <a href="/wiki/Dir_(clan)" title="Dir (clan)">Dir</a> clan, who enjoy the support of the <a href="/wiki/Somali_clan" title="Somali clan" class="mw-redirect">Somali clans</a>, especially the <a href="/wiki/Isaaq" title="Isaaq">Isaaq</a> (the clan of the current president's wife and many ministers &amp; government officials) and the <a href="/wiki/Gadabuursi" title="Gadabuursi">Gadabuursi</a> Dir (who are the third most prominent Somali clan in Djibouti politics). The country emerged from a decade-long <a href="/wiki/Djiboutian_Civil_War" title="Djiboutian Civil War">civil war</a> at the end of the 1990s, with the government and the <a href="/wiki/Front_for_the_Restoration_of_Unity_and_Democracy" title="Front for the Restoration of Unity and Democracy">Front for the Restoration of Unity and Democracy</a> (FRUD) signing a peace treaty in 2000. Two FRUD members subsequently joined the cabinet,<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> and beginning with <a href="/wiki/Djiboutian_presidential_election,_1999" title="Djiboutian presidential election, 1999">the presidential elections of 1999</a>, the FRUD has campaigned in support of the RPP.</p>
<p>Djibouti's current president, Guelleh, succeeded <a href="/wiki/Hassan_Gouled_Aptidon" title="Hassan Gouled Aptidon">Hassan Gouled Aptidon</a> in office in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-IRIN_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IRIN-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Guelleh was sworn in for his second six-year term after a <a href="/wiki/Djiboutian_presidential_election,_2005" title="Djiboutian presidential election, 2005">one-man election</a> on 8 April 2005. He took 100% of the votes in a 78.9% turnout.<sup id="cite_ref-IRIN_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IRIN-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> In early 2011, the Djiboutian citizenry took part in a <a href="/wiki/2011_Djiboutian_protests" title="2011 Djiboutian protests">series of protests</a> against the long-serving government, which were associated with the larger <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a> demonstrations. Guelleh was <a href="/wiki/Djiboutian_presidential_election,_2011" title="Djiboutian presidential election, 2011">re-elected</a> to a third term later that year, with 80.63% of the vote in a 75% turnout.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meodvpe_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meodvpe-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> Although opposition groups boycotted the ballot over changes to the constitution permitting Guelleh to run again for office,<sup id="cite_ref-Meodvpe_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meodvpe-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> international observers generally described the election as free and fair.<sup id="cite_ref-Spabcdpohr_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spabcdpohr-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On 31 March 2013, Guelleh replaced long-serving Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Dilleita_Mohamed_Dilleita" title="Dilleita Mohamed Dilleita" class="mw-redirect">Dilleita Mohamed Dilleita</a> with former president of the Union for a Presidential Majority (UMP) <a href="/wiki/Abdoulkader_Kamil_Mohamed" title="Abdoulkader Kamil Mohamed">Abdoulkader Kamil Mohamed</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Makmgcdlenpmd_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Makmgcdlenpmd-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> In December 2014, the ruling Union for the Presidential Majority also signed a framework agreement with the Union of National Salvation coalition, which paves the way for opposition legislators to enter parliament and for reformation of the national electoral agency.<sup id="cite_ref-Gdgrdtboip_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gdgrdtboip-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_and_foreign_relations">Military and foreign relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Military and foreign relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Djibouti_Armed_Forces" title="Djibouti Armed Forces">Djibouti Armed Forces</a>, <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_Djibouti" title="Military history of Djibouti">Military history of Djibouti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Djibouti" title="Foreign relations of Djibouti">Foreign relations of Djibouti</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Djibouti_Armed_Forces" title="Djibouti Armed Forces">Djibouti Armed Forces</a> include the Djibouti National Army, which consists of the Coastal Navy, the Djiboutian Air Force (Force Aerienne Djiboutienne, FAD), and the National Gendarmerie (GN). As of 2010, the manpower available for military service was 170,386 males and 221,411 females aged 16 to 49.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> Djibouti <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_military_expenditures" title="List of countries by military expenditures">spent</a> over $36 million USD annually on its military as of 2011 (141st in the <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_International_Peace_Research_Institute" title="Stockholm International Peace Research Institute">SIPRI</a> database).</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Djiboutian_soldiers_on_truck.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Djiboutian_soldiers_on_truck.jpg/220px-Djiboutian_soldiers_on_truck.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Djiboutian_soldiers_on_truck.jpg/330px-Djiboutian_soldiers_on_truck.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Djiboutian_soldiers_on_truck.jpg/440px-Djiboutian_soldiers_on_truck.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Djiboutian_soldiers_on_truck.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Military_of_Djibouti" title="Military of Djibouti" class="mw-redirect">Djiboutian Army</a> soldiers scanning the terrain during Operation Able Dart 08-01 on Forward Operating in <a href="/wiki/Ali_Sabieh" title="Ali Sabieh">Ali Sabieh</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first war which involved the Djiboutian Armed Forces was the <a href="/wiki/Djiboutian_Civil_War" title="Djiboutian Civil War">Djiboutian Civil War</a> between the Djiboutian government, supported by <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Front_for_the_Restoration_of_Unity_and_Democracy" title="Front for the Restoration of Unity and Democracy">Front for the Restoration of Unity and Democracy</a> (<i>FRUD</i>). The war lasted from 1991 to 2001, although most of the hostilities ended when the moderate factions of FRUD signed a peace treaty with the government after suffering an extensive military setback when the government forces captured most of the rebel-held territory. A radical group continued to fight the government, but signed its own peace treaty in 2001. The war ended in a government victory, and FRUD became a political party.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:El-Hajj_Hassan_Gouled_Military_Academy.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/El-Hajj_Hassan_Gouled_Military_Academy.png/220px-El-Hajj_Hassan_Gouled_Military_Academy.png" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/El-Hajj_Hassan_Gouled_Military_Academy.png/330px-El-Hajj_Hassan_Gouled_Military_Academy.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/El-Hajj_Hassan_Gouled_Military_Academy.png/440px-El-Hajj_Hassan_Gouled_Military_Academy.png 2x" data-file-width="727" data-file-height="561" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:El-Hajj_Hassan_Gouled_Military_Academy.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sign outside the El-Hajj Hassan Gouled Military Academy in <a href="/wiki/Holhol" title="Holhol">Holhol</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Djibouti has fought in clashes against <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Ras_Doumeira" title="Ras Doumeira" class="mw-redirect">Ras Doumeira</a> peninsula, which both countries claim to be under their sovereignty. The first clash occurred in 1996 after a nearly two-month stand-off. In 1999, a political crisis occurred when both sides accused each other for supporting its enemies. In 2008, the countries clashed again when Djibouti refused to return Eritrean deserters and Eritrea responded by firing at the Djiboutian forces.</p>
<p>As the headquarters of the IGAD regional body, Djibouti has been an active participant in the Somalian peace process, hosting the <a href="/wiki/Arta,_Djibouti" title="Arta, Djibouti">Arta</a> conference in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-Somvir_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Somvir-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Following the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Government_of_Somalia" title="Federal Government of Somalia">Federal Government of Somalia</a> in 2012,<sup id="cite_ref-Fcacsunesinpshm_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fcacsunesinpshm-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> a Djibouti delegation also attended the inauguration ceremony of Somalia's new president.<sup id="cite_ref-Piuinefs_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Piuinefs-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 2001, the Djiboutian government leased the former French military base <a href="/wiki/Camp_Lemonnier" title="Camp Lemonnier">Camp Lemonnier</a> to the <a href="/wiki/CENTCOM" title="CENTCOM" class="mw-redirect">United States Central Command</a> for operations related to <a href="/wiki/CJTF-HOA" title="CJTF-HOA" class="mw-redirect">Combined Joint Task Force Horn of Africa</a> (CJTF-HOA). In 2009, Central Command transitioned responsibilities in Africa to <a href="/wiki/United_States_Africa_Command" title="United States Africa Command">AFRICOM</a>. France's <a href="/wiki/13th_Demi-Brigade_of_the_Foreign_Legion" title="13th Demi-Brigade of the Foreign Legion">13th Demi-Brigade of the Foreign Legion</a> is based in Djibouti, but not in Djibouti City.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Administrative divisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Districts_of_Djibouti" title="Districts of Djibouti">Districts of Djibouti</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Djiboutimap.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Djiboutimap.png/220px-Djiboutimap.png" width="220" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Djiboutimap.png/330px-Djiboutimap.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Djiboutimap.png/440px-Djiboutimap.png 2x" data-file-width="489" data-file-height="504" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Djiboutimap.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of the regions of Djibouti.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Djibouti is sectioned into five administrative regions and one <a href="/wiki/Djibouti_(city)" title="Djibouti (city)">city</a>. It is further sub-divided into eleven <a href="/wiki/Districts_of_Djibouti" title="Districts of Djibouti">districts</a>.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Djibouti" title="Geography of Djibouti">Geography of Djibouti</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Location_and_habitat">Location and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Location and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gufa_volcanic_field.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Gufa_volcanic_field.jpg/220px-Gufa_volcanic_field.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Gufa_volcanic_field.jpg/330px-Gufa_volcanic_field.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Gufa_volcanic_field.jpg/440px-Gufa_volcanic_field.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="478" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gufa_volcanic_field.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Mousa_Ali_Volcano" title="Mousa Ali Volcano" class="mw-redirect">Mousa Ali Volcano</a>, Gufa volcanic field and pyroclastic cones.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Djibouti is situated in the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Aden" title="Gulf of Aden">Gulf of Aden</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bab-el-Mandeb" title="Bab-el-Mandeb">Bab-el-Mandeb</a>, at the southern entrance to the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>. It lies between latitudes 10° and 13°N, and longitudes 41° and 44°E. The country's coastline stretches 314 kilometres (195 miles), with terrain consisting mainly of plateaux, plains and highlands. Djibouti has a total area of 23,200 square kilometres (9,000&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> Its borders extend 506&#160;km (314&#160;mi), 113&#160;km (70&#160;mi) of which are shared with Eritrea, 337&#160;km (209&#160;mi) with Ethiopia, and 58&#160;km (36&#160;mi) with Somalia.</p>
<p>Djibouti has eight mountain ranges with peaks of over 1,000 m (3,281&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-Hmid_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hmid-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mousa_Ali" title="Mousa Ali">Mousa Ali</a> range is considered the country's highest mountain range, with the tallest peak on the border with Ethiopia and Eritrea. It has an elevation of 2,028 m.<sup id="cite_ref-Hmid_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hmid-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Grand_Bara" title="Grand Bara">Grand Bara</a> desert covers parts of southern Djibouti in the Arta, Ali Sabieh and Dikhil regions. The majority of it sits at a relatively low elevation, below 1,700 feet (560 m).</p>
<p>Extreme geographic points include: to the north, Ras Doumera and the point at which the border with Eritrea enters the Red Sea in the Obock Region; to the east, a section of the Red Sea coast north of Ras Bir; to the south, a location on the border with Ethiopia west of the town of <a href="/wiki/As_Ela" title="As Ela" class="mw-redirect">As Ela</a>; and to the west, a location on the frontier with Ethiopia immediately east of the Ethiopian town of <a href="/wiki/Afambo" title="Afambo" class="mw-redirect">Afambo</a>.</p>
<p>Most of Djibouti is part of the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_xeric_grasslands_and_shrublands" title="Ethiopian xeric grasslands and shrublands">Ethiopian xeric grasslands and shrublands</a> <a href="/wiki/Ecoregion" title="Ecoregion">ecoregion</a>. The exception is a strip along the Red Sea coast, which is part of the <a href="/wiki/Eritrean_coastal_desert" title="Eritrean coastal desert">Eritrean coastal desert</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Djib_003.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Djib_003.JPG/220px-Djib_003.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Djib_003.JPG/330px-Djib_003.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Djib_003.JPG/440px-Djib_003.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Djib_003.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Lake_Assal_(Djibouti)" title="Lake Assal (Djibouti)">Lake Assal</a> area</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There is not much seasonal variation in Djibouti's <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a>. Hot conditions prevail year-round along with winter rainfalls. Mean daily maximum temperatures range from 32 to 41&#160;°C (90 to 106&#160;°F), except at high elevations, where the effects of a cold offshore current can be felt. In <a href="/wiki/Djibouti_city" title="Djibouti city" class="mw-redirect">Djibouti city</a>, for instance, average afternoon highs range from 28 to 34&#160;°C (82 to 93&#160;°F) in April. Nationally, mean daily minimums usually vary from 15 to 30&#160;°C (59 to 86&#160;°F).<sup id="cite_ref-climate_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-climate-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> The greatest range in climate occurs in eastern Djibouti, where temperatures sometimes surpass 41&#160;°C (106&#160;°F) in July on the littoral plains and the freezing point during December in the highlands.<sup id="cite_ref-climate_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-climate-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> In this region, relative humidity ranges from about 40% in the mid-afternoon to 85% at night, changing somewhat according to the season.</p>
<p>Djibouti's climate ranges from arid in the northeastern coastal regions to <a href="/wiki/Semiarid" title="Semiarid" class="mw-redirect">semiarid</a> in the central, northern, western and southern parts of the country. On the eastern seaboard, annual rainfall is less than 5 inches (131&#160;mm); in the central highlands, precipitation is about 8 to 11 inches (200 to 300&#160;mm). Although the coastal regions are hot and humid throughout the year, the hinterland is typically hot and dry. The climate on the coast is mild, with wet winters and warm, sunny and rather dry summers.</p>
