<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wildlife">Wildlife</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Wildlife">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Djibouti" title="Wildlife of Djibouti">Wildlife of Djibouti</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Urocolius_macrourus-20090110B.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Urocolius_macrourus-20090110B.jpg/130px-Urocolius_macrourus-20090110B.jpg" width="130" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Urocolius_macrourus-20090110B.jpg/195px-Urocolius_macrourus-20090110B.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Urocolius_macrourus-20090110B.jpg/260px-Urocolius_macrourus-20090110B.jpg 2x" data-file-width="579" data-file-height="756" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Urocolius_macrourus-20090110B.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Blue-naped_mousebird" title="Blue-naped mousebird">blue-naped mousebird</a> (<i>Urocolius macrourus</i>), a common bird species in Djibouti.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The country's wildlife, consisting of <a href="/wiki/Flora" title="Flora">flora</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fauna" title="Fauna">fauna</a>, is in a harsh landscape with forest accounting for less than one percent of the total area of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-day_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-day-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> Wildlife is spread over three main regions namely, from the northern mountain region of the country to the volcanic plateaux in its southern and central part and culminating in the coastal region.</p>
<p>The flora and fauna species are mostly found in the northern part of the country, in the ecosystem of the <a href="/wiki/Day_Forest_National_Park" title="Day Forest National Park">Day Forest National Park</a>. At an average altitude of 1,500 metres (4,921 feet), the area includes the Goda massif, with a peak of 1,783&#160;m (5,850&#160;ft). It covers an area of 3.5 square kilometres (1&#160;sq&#160;mi) of <a href="/wiki/Juniperus_procera" title="Juniperus procera">Juniperus procera</a> forest, with many of the trees rising to 20 metres (66 feet) height. This forest area is the main habitat of the endangered and endemic <a href="/wiki/Djibouti_francolin" title="Djibouti francolin">Djibouti francolin</a> (a bird), and another recently noted vertebrate, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Platyceps_afarensis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Platyceps afarensis (page does not exist)">Platyceps afarensis</a></i> (a <a href="/wiki/Colubrine" title="Colubrine" class="mw-redirect">colubrine</a> snake). It also contains many species of woody and herbaceous plants, including boxwood and olive trees, which account for 60% of the total identified species in the country.</p>
<p>According to the country profile related to biodiversity of wildlife in Djibouti, the nation contains some 820 species of plants, 493 species of invertebrates, 455 species of fish, 40 species of reptiles, 3 species of amphibians, 360 species of birds and 66 species of mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-day_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-day-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> Wildlife of Djibouti is also listed as part of <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_hotspot" title="Biodiversity hotspot">biodiversity hotspot</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Aden" title="Gulf of Aden">Gulf of Aden</a> <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reef</a> hotspot.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Djibouti" title="Economy of Djibouti">Economy of Djibouti</a></div>
<div class="hatnote boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_companies_based_in_Djibouti" title="List of companies based in Djibouti">List of companies based in Djibouti</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Djibouti_Port.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Djibouti_Port.JPG/220px-Djibouti_Port.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Djibouti_Port.JPG/330px-Djibouti_Port.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Djibouti_Port.JPG/440px-Djibouti_Port.JPG 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Djibouti_Port.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fishing boats docked at the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Djibouti" title="Port of Djibouti">Port of Djibouti</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Djibouti's economy is largely concentrated in the service sector. Commercial activities revolve around the country's free trade policies and strategic location as a Red Sea transit point. Due to limited rainfall, vegetables and fruits serve as the principal production crops, and other food items require importation. The GDP (purchasing power parity) in 2013 was estimated at $2.505 billion, with a real growth rate of 5% annually. Per capita income is around $2,874 (PPP). The services sector constituted around 79.7% of the GDP, followed by industry at 17.3%, and agriculture at 3%.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2013, the container terminal at the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Djibouti" title="Port of Djibouti">Port of Djibouti</a> handles the bulk of the nation's trade. About 70% of the seaport's activity consists of imports to and exports from neighboring <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, which depends on the harbour as its main maritime outlet. The port also serves as an international refueling center and transshipment hub.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2012, the Djiboutian government in collaboration with DP World started construction on the Doraleh Container Terminal,<sup id="cite_ref-Cdpafoid_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cdpafoid-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> a third major seaport intended to further develop the national transit capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> A $396 million project, it has the capacity to accommodate 1.5 million twenty foot container units annually.<sup id="cite_ref-Cdpafoid_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cdpafoid-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Djibouti was ranked the 177th safest investment destination in the world in the March 2011 Euromoney Country Risk rankings.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> To improve the environment for direct foreign investment, the Djibouti authorities in conjunction with various non-profit organizations have launched a number of development projects aimed at highlighting the country's commercial potential. The government has also introduced new private sector policies targeting high interest and inflation rates, including relaxing the tax burden on enterprises and allowing for exemptions on consumption tax.<sup id="cite_ref-Cdpafoid_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cdpafoid-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tree_map_export_2009_Djibouti.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Tree_map_export_2009_Djibouti.jpeg/220px-Tree_map_export_2009_Djibouti.jpeg" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Tree_map_export_2009_Djibouti.jpeg/330px-Tree_map_export_2009_Djibouti.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Tree_map_export_2009_Djibouti.jpeg/440px-Tree_map_export_2009_Djibouti.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1103" data-file-height="862" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tree_map_export_2009_Djibouti.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Graphical depiction of Djibouti's product exports in 28 color-coded categories.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Additionally, efforts have been made to lower the estimated 60% urban unemployment rate by creating more job opportunities through investment in diversified sectors. Funds have especially gone toward building telecommunications infrastructure and increasing disposable income by supporting small businesses. Owing to its growth potential, the fishing and agro-processing sector, which represents around 15% of GDP, has also enjoyed rising investment since 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-Cdpafoid_49-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cdpafoid-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>To expand the modest industrial sector, a 56 megawatt geothermal power plant slated to be completed by 2018 is being constructed with the help of <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a>, the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Global_Environmental_Facility" title="Global Environmental Facility" class="mw-redirect">Global Environmental Facility</a>. The facility is expected to solve the recurring issue of electricity shortages, decrease the nation's reliance on Ethiopia for energy, reduce costly oil imports for diesel-generated electricity, and thereby buttress the GDP and lower debt.<sup id="cite_ref-Cdpafoid_49-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cdpafoid-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Algamildj.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Algamildj.jpeg/220px-Algamildj.jpeg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/59/Algamildj.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/59/Algamildj.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="199" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Algamildj.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A truck operator at <a href="/wiki/Al_Gamil" title="Al Gamil">Al Gamil</a>, the largest construction company in Djibouti</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Djibouti firm Salt Investment (SIS) began a large-scale operation to industrialize the plentiful salt in Djibouti's <a href="/wiki/Lake_Assal_(Djibouti)" title="Lake Assal (Djibouti)">Lake Assal</a> region. Operating at an annual capacity of 4 million tons, the desalination project has lifted export revenues, created more job opportunities, and provided more fresh water for the area's residents.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cdpafoid_49-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cdpafoid-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2012, the Djibouti government also enlisted the services of the China Harbor Engineering Company Ltd for the construction of an ore terminal in the area. Worth $64 million, the project is scheduled to be completed within two years and will enable Djibouti mineral officials to export a further 5 thousand tons of salt per year to markets in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Xdcssmuatfse_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Xdcssmuatfse-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Djiboutian_franc" title="Djiboutian franc">Djiboutian franc</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Currency" title="Currency">currency</a> of Djibouti. It is issued by the <a href="/wiki/Central_Bank_of_Djibouti" title="Central Bank of Djibouti">Central Bank of Djibouti</a>, the country's <a href="/wiki/Monetary_authority" title="Monetary authority">monetary authority</a>. Since the Djiboutian franc is pegged to the <a href="/wiki/U.S._dollar" title="U.S. dollar" class="mw-redirect">U.S. dollar</a>, it is generally stable and inflation is not a problem. This has contributed to the growing interest in investment in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Cdpafoid_49-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cdpafoid-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rdbbafi_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rdbbafi-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2010, 10 conventional and Islamic banks operate in Djibouti. Most arrived within the past few years, including the Somali money transfer company <a href="/wiki/Dahabshiil" title="Dahabshiil">Dahabshiil</a> and BDCD, a subsidiary of Swiss Financial Investments. The banking system had heretofore been monopolized by two institutions, the Indo-Suez Bank and the Commercial and Industrial Bank (BCIMR).<sup id="cite_ref-Rdbbafi_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rdbbafi-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> To assure a robust credit and deposit sector, the government requires commercial banks to maintain 30% of shares in the financial institution; a minimum of 300 million Djiboutian francs in up front capital is mandatory for international banks. Lending has likewise been encouraged by the creation of a guarantee fund, which allows banks to issue loans to eligible small-and-medium-sized businesses without first requiring a large deposit or other collateral.<sup id="cite_ref-Cdpafoid_49-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cdpafoid-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Saudi investors are also reportedly exploring the possibility of linking the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> via an 18-mile (29&#160;km) long oversea bridge through Djibouti referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Bridge_of_the_Horns" title="Bridge of the Horns">Bridge of the Horns</a>. The investor <a href="/wiki/Tarek_bin_Laden" title="Tarek bin Laden">Tarek bin Laden</a> has been linked to the project.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Djibouti" title="Demographics of Djibouti">Demographics of Djibouti</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:A_Somali_man.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/A_Somali_man.jpeg/150px-A_Somali_man.jpeg" width="150" height="181" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/A_Somali_man.jpeg/225px-A_Somali_man.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/A_Somali_man.jpeg/300px-A_Somali_man.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="583" data-file-height="704" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">A <a href="/wiki/Somali_people" title="Somali people" class="mw-redirect">Somali</a> man in a traditional <a href="/wiki/Taqiyah_(cap)" title="Taqiyah (cap)">taqiyah</a>.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:An_Afar_nomad.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/An_Afar_nomad.jpg/150px-An_Afar_nomad.jpg" width="150" height="212" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/An_Afar_nomad.jpg/225px-An_Afar_nomad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/An_Afar_nomad.jpg/300px-An_Afar_nomad.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1128" data-file-height="1596" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">An <a href="/wiki/Afar_people" title="Afar people">Afar</a> man in nomadic attire.</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Djibouti is a <a href="/wiki/Multiethnic" title="Multiethnic" class="mw-redirect">multiethnic</a> country. The two largest ethnic groups are the <a href="/wiki/Somali_people" title="Somali people" class="mw-redirect">Somali</a> (60%) and the <a href="/wiki/Afar_people" title="Afar people">Afar</a> (35%). The <a href="/wiki/Somali_clan" title="Somali clan" class="mw-redirect">Somali clan</a> component is mainly composed of the <a href="/wiki/Issa_(clan)" title="Issa (clan)">Issas</a>, a sub-clan of the larger <a href="/wiki/Dir_(clan)" title="Dir (clan)">Dir</a>. The remaining 5% of Djibouti's population primarily consists of <a href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab" class="mw-redirect">Arabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europeans</a> (<a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italian_people" title="Italian people" class="mw-redirect">Italians</a>). Approximately 76% of local residents are <a href="/wiki/Urban_area" title="Urban area">urban</a> dwellers; the remainder are <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">pastoralists</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In addition, Djibouti is a <a href="/wiki/Multilingual" title="Multilingual" class="mw-redirect">multilingual</a> nation, with a population of about 774,389 inhabitants.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> According to <i><a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">Ethnologue</a></i>, the majority of the residents speak <a href="/wiki/Somali_language" title="Somali language">Somali</a> (297,000 speakers) or <a href="/wiki/Afar_language" title="Afar language">Afar</a> (99,200 speakers) as a first language, which are the <a href="/wiki/Mother_tongue" title="Mother tongue" class="mw-redirect">mother tongues</a> of the Somali and Afar ethnic groups, respectively. Both languages belong to the larger <a href="/wiki/Afro-Asiatic_languages" title="Afro-Asiatic languages" class="mw-redirect">Afro-Asiatic</a> family. There are two official languages in Djibouti: <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> (Afro-Asiatic) and <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> (<a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a>). Arabic is of social, cultural and religious importance. In formal settings, it consists of <a href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic" title="Modern Standard Arabic">Modern Standard Arabic</a>. Colloquially, about 36,000 local residents speak the <a href="/wiki/Ta%27izzi-Adeni_Arabic" title="Ta'izzi-Adeni Arabic">Ta'izzi-Adeni Arabic</a> dialect, also known as <i>Djibouti Arabic</i>. French was inherited from the colonial period and is the primary language of instruction. About 10,200 Djiboutians speak it as a first language. Immigrant languages include <a href="/wiki/Omani_Arabic" title="Omani Arabic">Omani Arabic</a> (38,900 speakers), <a href="/wiki/Amharic_language" title="Amharic language" class="mw-redirect">Amharic</a> (1,400 speakers), <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> (1,000 speakers) and <a href="/wiki/Hindi_language" title="Hindi language" class="mw-redirect">Hindi</a> (600 speakers).<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Djibouti" title="Education in Djibouti">Education in Djibouti</a></div>
<p>Education is a priority for the government of Djibouti. As of 2009, it allocates 20.5% of its annual budget to scholastic instruction.<sup id="cite_ref-infodev.org_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infodev.org-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Djibwomglopul.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Djibwomglopul.jpeg/220px-Djibwomglopul.jpeg" width="220" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/df/Djibwomglopul.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/df/Djibwomglopul.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="265" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Djibwomglopul.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Djiboutian women participating in the Global Pulse educational initiative (2010).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Djiboutian educational system was initially formulated to cater to a limited pupil base. As such, the schooling framework was largely elitist and drew considerably from the French colonial paradigm, which was ill-suited to local circumstances and needs.<sup id="cite_ref-infodev.org_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infodev.org-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the late 1990s, the Djiboutian authorities revised the national educational strategy and launched a broad-based consultative process involving administrative officials, teachers, parents, national assembly members and NGOs. The initiative identified areas in need of attention and produced concrete recommendations on how to go about improving them. The government subsequently prepared a comprehensive reform plan aimed at modernizing the educational sector over the 2000–10 period. In August 2000, it passed an official Education Planning Act and drafted a medium-term development plan for the next five years. The fundamental academic system was significantly restructured and made compulsory; it now consists of five years of primary school and four years of middle school. Secondary schools also require a Certificate of Fundamental Education for admission. In addition, the new law introduced secondary-level vocational instruction and established university facilities in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-infodev.org_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infodev.org-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As a result of the Education Planning Act and the medium-term action strategy, substantial progress has been registered throughout the educational sector.<sup id="cite_ref-infodev.org_55-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infodev.org-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> In particular, school enrollment, attendance and retention rates have all steadily increased, with some regional variation. From 2004–05 to 2007–08, net enrollments of girls in primary school rose by 18.6%; for boys, it increased 8.0%. Net enrollments in middle school over the same period rose by 72.4% for girls and 52.2% for boys. At the secondary level, the rate of increase in net enrollments was 49.8% for girls and 56.1% for boys.<sup id="cite_ref-Datee_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Datee-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Djiboutian government has especially focused on developing and improving institutional infrastructure and teaching materials, including constructing new classrooms and supplying textbooks. At the post-secondary level, emphasis has also been placed on producing qualified instructors and encouraging out-of-school youngsters to pursue vocational training.<sup id="cite_ref-infodev.org_55-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infodev.org-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> As of 2012, the literacy rate in Djibouti was estimated at 70%.<sup id="cite_ref-Pcaotw_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pcaotw-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Institutions of higher learning in the country include the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Djibouti" title="University of Djibouti">University of Djibouti</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Djibouti" title="Health in Djibouti">Health in Djibouti</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Djibsilay.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Djibsilay.jpeg/220px-Djibsilay.jpeg" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ed/Djibsilay.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ed/Djibsilay.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="254" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Djibsilay.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Djibouti government officials concluding an agreement with US representatives for the development of the national Ministry of Health (2008).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> at birth is about 62 for both females and males. Fertility is at 2.63 children per woman.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> In the country there are about 18 doctors per 100,000 persons.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In June 2011, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Population_Fund" title="United Nations Population Fund">United Nations Population Fund</a> released a report on <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.unfpa.org/sowmy/report/home.html">The State of the World's Midwifery</a>. It contained new data on the midwifery workforce and policies relating to newborn and maternal mortality for 58 countries. The 2010 maternal mortality rate per 100,000 births for Djibouti is 300. This is compared with 461.6 in 2008 and 606.5 in 1990. The under 5 mortality rate, per 1,000 births is 95 and the neonatal mortality as a percentage of under 5's mortality is 37. The aim of this report is to highlight ways in which the <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Development_Goals" title="Millennium Development Goals">Millennium Development Goals</a> can be achieved, particularly Goal 4 – Reduce child mortality and Goal 5 – improve maternal death. In Djibouti the number of midwives per 1,000 live births is 6 and the lifetime risk of death for pregnant women 1 in 93.<sup id="cite_ref-SOWMY_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SOWMY-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>According to a 2005 World Health Organization estimate, about 93.1% of Djibouti's women and girls have undergone <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a> (female circumcision),<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> a pre-marital custom mainly endemic to Northeast Africa and parts of the Near East that has its ultimate origins in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rohayes_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rohayes-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bodman_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bodman-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> Although legally proscribed in 1994, the procedure is still widely practiced, as it is deeply ingrained in the local culture.<sup id="cite_ref-Djiwfm_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Djiwfm-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> Encouraged and performed by women in the community, circumcision is primarily intended to deter promiscuity and to offer protection from assault.<sup id="cite_ref-Djiwfm_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Djiwfm-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Frayser_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frayser-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> About 94% of Djibouti's male population has also reportedly undergone <a href="/wiki/Circumcision" title="Circumcision">male circumcision</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ahuja_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahuja-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Djibouti" title="Islam in Djibouti">Islam in Djibouti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Djibouti" title="Christianity in Djibouti">Christianity in Djibouti</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Djibouti_mosque.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Djibouti_mosque.JPG/220px-Djibouti_mosque.JPG" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Djibouti_mosque.JPG/330px-Djibouti_mosque.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Djibouti_mosque.JPG/440px-Djibouti_mosque.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1295" data-file-height="1018" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Djibouti_mosque.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The emblematic Hamoudi Mosque of Djibouti City.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Djibouti's population is predominantly <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a>. <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> is observed by 94% of the nation's population (around 740,000 as of 2012), while the remaining 6% of residents are <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christians</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="barbox tright" style="border:1px solid silver; font-size:88%; padding:0.4em; width:auto; background: white;">
<table style="border-collapse:collapse; width:100%;">
<tr style="background:#ddd">
<th colspan="5">Religion in Djibouti<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></th>
</tr>
<tr style="font-size:88%; height:4px;">
<td style="padding:0 4px;">religion</td>
<td style="padding:0 4px; text-align:right;"></td>
<td style="width:100px;"></td>
<td style="padding:0 4px; width:1em; text-align:right;">percent</td>
<td style="padding:0 4px; text-align:right;"></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;"><a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Djibouti" title="Islam in Djibouti">Islam</a></td>
<td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;">
<div style="background:green; width:94%; overflow: hidden;">&#160; </div>
</td>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">94%</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;"><a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Djibouti" title="Christianity in Djibouti">Christianity</a></td>
<td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;">
<div style="background:purple; width:6%; overflow: hidden;">&#160; </div>
</td>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">6%</td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
<p>The Constitution of Djibouti names Islam as the sole <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">state religion</a>, and also provides for the equality of citizens of all faiths (Article 1) and freedom of religious practice (Article 11).<sup id="cite_ref-constitution_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-constitution-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> Most local Muslims adhere to the <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni</a> denomination, following the <a href="/wiki/Shafi%27i" title="Shafi'i">Shafi'i</a> school. The <a href="/wiki/Non-denominational_Muslim" title="Non-denominational Muslim" class="mw-redirect">non-denominational Muslims</a> largely belong to <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a> orders of varying schools.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Religious_Freedom_Report" title="International Religious Freedom Report" class="mw-redirect">International Religious Freedom Report</a> 2008, while Muslim Djiboutians have the legal right to convert to or marry someone from another faith, converts may encounter negative reactions from their family and clan or from society at large, and they often face pressure to go back to Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Diocese_of_Djibouti" title="Roman Catholic Diocese of Djibouti">Diocese of Djibouti</a> serves the small local <a href="/wiki/Catholicism" title="Catholicism">Catholic</a> population, which it estimates numbered around 7,000 individuals in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Djibouti" title="Culture of Djibouti">Culture of Djibouti</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wood_jar_from_Oue%60a,_Tadjoura,_Djibouti.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Wood_jar_from_Oue%60a%2C_Tadjoura%2C_Djibouti.jpg/130px-Wood_jar_from_Oue%60a%2C_Tadjoura%2C_Djibouti.jpg" width="130" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Wood_jar_from_Oue%60a%2C_Tadjoura%2C_Djibouti.jpg/195px-Wood_jar_from_Oue%60a%2C_Tadjoura%2C_Djibouti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Wood_jar_from_Oue%60a%2C_Tadjoura%2C_Djibouti.jpg/260px-Wood_jar_from_Oue%60a%2C_Tadjoura%2C_Djibouti.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2736" data-file-height="3648" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wood_jar_from_Oue%60a,_Tadjoura,_Djibouti.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Traditional wood-carved jar from <a href="/wiki/Oued_Ouea" title="Oued Ouea">Oue`a</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Tadjourah_Region" title="Tadjourah Region">Tadjourah</a> region.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Djiboutian attire reflects the region's hot and arid climate. When not dressed in western clothing such as jeans and T-shirts, men typically wear the <i>macawiis</i>, which is a <a href="/wiki/Sarong" title="Sarong">sarong</a>-like garment worn around the waist. Among nomads, many wear a loosely wrapped white cotton robe called a <i>tobe</i> that goes down to about the knee, with the end thrown over the shoulder (much like a Roman <a href="/wiki/Toga" title="Toga">toga</a>).</p>
<p>Women typically wear the <i>dirac</i>, which is a long, light, diaphanous <a href="/wiki/Voile" title="Voile">voile</a> dress made of cotton or polyester that is worn over a full-length <a href="/wiki/Slip_(clothing)" title="Slip (clothing)">half-slip</a> and a bra. Married women tend to sport head-scarves referred to as <i>shash</i>, and also often cover their upper body with a <a href="/wiki/Shawl" title="Shawl">shawl</a> known as <i>garbasaar</i>. Unmarried or young women, however, do not always cover their heads. Traditional Arabian garb such as the male <a href="/wiki/Jellabiya" title="Jellabiya">jellabiya</a> (<i>jellabiyaad</i> in Somali) and the female <a href="/wiki/Jilb%C4%81b" title="Jilbāb">jilbāb</a> is also commonly worn. For some occasions such as festivals, women may adorn themselves with specialized jewelry and head-dresses similar to those worn by the <a href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people" class="mw-redirect">Berber</a> tribes of the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A lot of Djibouti's original art is passed on and preserved orally, mainly through song. Many examples of Islamic, Ottoman, and French influences can also be noted in the local buildings, which contain plasterwork, carefully constructed <a href="/wiki/Motif_(visual_arts)" title="Motif (visual arts)">motifs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">calligraphy</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Djibouti" title="Music of Djibouti">Music of Djibouti</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oud.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Oud.jpg/150px-Oud.jpg" width="150" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Oud.jpg/225px-Oud.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a1/Oud.jpg 2x" data-file-width="231" data-file-height="384" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oud.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <i><a href="/wiki/Oud" title="Oud">oud</a></i> is a staple instrument in Djibouti's traditional music.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Somalis have a rich musical heritage centered on traditional Somali <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folklore</a>. Most Somali songs are <a href="/wiki/Pentatonic_scale" title="Pentatonic scale">pentatonic</a>. That is, they only use five <a href="/wiki/Pitch_(music)" title="Pitch (music)">pitches</a> per <a href="/wiki/Octave" title="Octave">octave</a> in contrast to a <a href="/wiki/Heptatonic_scale" title="Heptatonic scale">heptatonic</a> (seven note) scale such as the <a href="/wiki/Major_scale" title="Major scale">major scale</a>. At first listen, Somali music might be mistaken for the sounds of nearby regions such as Ethiopia, <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>, but it is ultimately recognizable by its own unique tunes and styles. Somali songs are usually the product of collaboration between <a href="/wiki/Lyricist" title="Lyricist">lyricists</a> (<i>midho</i>), <a href="/wiki/Songwriter" title="Songwriter">songwriters</a> (<i>laxan</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Singing" title="Singing">singers</a> (<i>codka</i> or "voice"). <a href="/wiki/Balwo" title="Balwo">Balwo</a> is a Somali musical style centered on love themes that is popular in Djibouti.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Traditional Afar music resembles the folk music of other parts of the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Ethiopia" title="Music of Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>; it also contains elements of <a href="/wiki/Arabic_music" title="Arabic music">Arabic music</a>. The history of Djibouti is recorded in the poetry and songs of its nomadic people, and goes back thousands of years to a time when the peoples of Djibouti traded hides and skins for the perfumes and spices of ancient <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. Afar oral literature is also quite musical. It comes in many varieties, including songs for weddings, war, praise and boasting.<sup id="cite_ref-expedition_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-expedition-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Literature_of_Djibouti" title="Literature of Djibouti">Literature of Djibouti</a></div>
<p>Djibouti has a long tradition of poetry. Several well-developed Somali forms of verse include the <i>gabay</i>, <i>jiifto</i>, <i>geeraar</i>, <i>wiglo</i>, <i>buraanbur</i>, <i>beercade</i>, <i>afarey</i> and <i>guuraw</i>. The gabay (epic poem) has the most complex length and meter, often exceeding 100 lines. It is considered the mark of poetic attainment when a young poet is able to compose such verse, and is regarded as the height of poetry. Groups of memorizers and reciters (<i>hafidayaal</i>) traditionally propagated the well-developed art form. Poems revolve around several main themes, including <i>baroorodiiq</i> (elegy), <i>amaan</i> (praise), <i>jacayl</i> (romance), <i>guhaadin</i> (diatribe), <i>digasho</i> (gloating) and <i>guubaabo</i> (guidance). The baroorodiiq is composed to commemorate the death of a prominent poet or figure.<sup id="cite_ref-Abdullahi75_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdullahi75-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> The Afar are familiar with the <i>ginnili</i>, a kind of warrior-poet and diviner, and have a rich oral tradition of folk stories. They also have an extensive repertoire of battle songs.<sup id="cite_ref-Phillips_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Additionally, Djibouti has a long tradition of Islamic literature. Among the most prominent such historical works is the medieval <i>Futuh Al-Habash</i> by Shihāb al-Dīn, which chronicles the <a href="/wiki/Adal_Sultanate" title="Adal Sultanate">Adal Sultanate</a> army's <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian-Adal_War" title="Ethiopian-Adal War" class="mw-redirect">conquest of Abyssinia</a> during the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Shihab_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shihab-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> In recent years, a number of politicians and intellectuals have also penned memoirs or reflections on the country.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Cuisine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Djiboutian_cuisine" title="Djiboutian cuisine">Djiboutian cuisine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sambusadish.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Sambusadish.jpg/170px-Sambusadish.jpg" width="170" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Sambusadish.jpg/255px-Sambusadish.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Sambusadish.jpg/340px-Sambusadish.jpg 2x" data-file-width="498" data-file-height="374" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sambusadish.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A plate of <i>sambusas</i> (<a href="/wiki/Samosa" title="Samosa">samosas</a>), a popular traditional snack.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Djiboutian_cuisine" title="Djiboutian cuisine">Djiboutian cuisine</a> consists of a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Somali_cuisine" title="Somali cuisine">Somali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afar_people" title="Afar people">Afar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_cuisine" title="Ethiopian cuisine">Ethiopian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yemeni_cuisine" title="Yemeni cuisine">Yemeni</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_cuisine" title="French cuisine">French cuisine</a>, with some additional <a href="/wiki/Asian_cuisine" title="Asian cuisine">Asian</a> (especially <a href="/wiki/Indian_cuisine" title="Indian cuisine">Indian</a>) culinary influences. Local dishes are commonly prepared using a lot of Middle Eastern spices, ranging from <a href="/wiki/Saffron" title="Saffron">saffron</a> to <a href="/wiki/Cinnamon" title="Cinnamon">cinnamon</a>. Spicy dishes come in many variations, from the traditional <i>Fah-fah</i> or "Soupe Djiboutienne" (spicy boiled beef soup) to the <i>yetakelt wet</i> (spicy mixed vegetable stew). <i>Xalwo</i> (halwo) or <a href="/wiki/Halva" title="Halva">halva</a> is a popular confection eaten during festive occasions, such as <a href="/wiki/Eid_ul-Fitr" title="Eid ul-Fitr" class="mw-redirect">Eid</a> celebrations or wedding receptions. It is made from <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corn_starch" title="Corn starch">corn starch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardamom" title="Cardamom">cardamom</a> powder, <a href="/wiki/Nutmeg" title="Nutmeg">nutmeg</a> powder and <a href="/wiki/Ghee" title="Ghee">ghee</a>. <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">Peanuts</a> are also sometimes added to enhance texture and flavor.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> After meals, homes are traditionally perfumed using <a href="/wiki/Incense" title="Incense">incense</a> (<i>cuunsi</i>) or <a href="/wiki/Frankincense" title="Frankincense">frankincense</a> (<i>lubaan</i>), which is prepared inside an incense burner referred to as a <i><a href="/wiki/Dabqaad" title="Dabqaad">dabqaad</a></i>.</p>
